واذا وكل الحر العاقل البالغ او الماذون مثلهما جاز لان المؤکل مالك للتصرف والوكيل من اهل العبارة وان وكل صبيا محجورا يعقل البيع والشراء او عبـدا مـحـجـورا جاز ولا يتعلق بهما الحقوق وتتعلق بمؤ كلهما لان الصبي من اهل العبارة الا ترى انه ينفذ تصرفه باذن وليه والعبد من اهل التصرف على نفسه مالک له وانما لا يملكه فى حق المولى والتوكيل ليس تصرفا فى حقه الا انه لا يصح منهما التزام العهدة اما الصبي لقصور اهليته والعبد لحق سيده فتلزم المؤكل وعن ابى يوسف ان المشترى اذا لم يعلم بحال البائع ثم علم انه صبى او مجنون او محجور له خيار الفسخ لانه دخل فى العقد على ظن ان حقوقه تتعلق بالعاقد فاذا ظهر خلافه يتخير كما اذا عثر على عيب

ترجمہ اور اگر آزاد عاقل، بالغ نے یا مازون نے اپنے مثل کو وکیل کیا تو جائز ہے اسلئے کہ موکل تصرف کا مالک ہے اور وکیل اہل عبارت میں سے ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے مجور بچہ کوجو بیع اور شراء کو سجھتا ہے یا غلام مجو کو وکیل کیا تو جائز ہے اور ان دونوں کے ساتھ حقوق متعلق نہیں ہوں گے۔ البتہ ان کے موکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے۔ اسلئے کہ بچہ اہل عبارت میں سے ہے۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ عاقل بچہ کا تصرف اسکے ولی کی اجازت سے نافذ ہو جاتا ہے اور غلام اپنی ذات پر تصرف کی لیاقت رکھتا ہے۔ تصرف کا مالک ہے صرف اس کو مولیٰ کے حق میں تصرف کا اختیار نہیں ہے۔ اور تو کیل مولیٰ کے حق میں تصرف نہیں ہے۔ مگر ان دونوں کی طرف سے عہدہ لا زم تو صحیح نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ بہر حال بچہ تو اس کی ولایت قاصر ہونے کی وجہ سے اور غلام اس کے مولیٰ کے حق کی وجہ سے پس ( یہ حقوق ) مؤکل کے ذمہ لازم ہوں گے اور ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے کہ مشتری اگر بائع کا حال نہ جانتا ہو پھر اس کو معلوم ہوا کہ وہ بچہ یا مجنون یا مجور غلام ہے تو اس کیلئے بیع فسخ کرنے کا اختیار ہے۔ اسلئے کہ مشتری عقد میں اس گمان کے ساتھ داخل ہوا تھا کہ اس کے حقوق عاقد کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں ۔ پس جب اس کے خلاف ظاہر ہوا تو وہ مختار ہو گا جیسے جب وہ صبیع کے عیب پر مطلع ہوا ہو۔
Second condition that must exist in the agent: that the agent be a person who understands the meaning and concept of the contract, i.e., he knows that 'sale is a divestiture and purchase is an acquisition'—meaning that by sale the ownership of the seller is divested from the sold item and by purchase ownership of the sold item is acquired for the buyer—and he is aware of [deception] and gross fraud, and the agent intends this contract, i.e., he is not one who acts negligently but rather one who intends. The proof is that in effecting expression and speech, the agent stands in place of the principal; therefore, it is necessary and a condition that the agent possess the capacity and qualification for expression, and since the capacity for expression is through sound intellect, the agent must be sane. Consequently, if the agent is an insane child or a madman, then the agency is invalid because no ruling is attached to their speech.

"And if a free, sane, adult, or a permitted [slave] appoints one like them as agent, it is permissible because the principal owns the right to transact and the agent is among those capable of expression. And if he appoints a restricted child who understands sale and purchase, or a restricted slave, it is permissible, but rights are not attached to them; rather they attach to their principal, because the child is among those capable of expression; do you not see that his transaction is effective with the permission of his guardian? And the slave has capacity for transaction over himself, he owns it, only he does not possess it with respect to his master, and appointment as agent is not a transaction concerning his master's right. However, they cannot validly undertake liability: the child due to deficiency of his capacity, and the slave due to the right of his master. So liability falls upon the principal. And from Abu Yusuf: that if the buyer did not know the condition of the seller and then learned that he was a child, a madman, or a restricted [slave], he has the option to rescind, because he entered into the contract under the assumption that his rights would attach to the contracting party; when the opposite appears, he has the choice, as when he discovers a defect."

Translation: And if a free, sane, adult or a permitted [slave] appoints one like them as agent, it is permissible because the principal owns the right to transact and the agent is among those capable of expression. And if he appoints a restricted child who understands sale and purchase, or a restricted slave, it is permissible, and rights will not be attached to them; rather they will be attached to their principal. Because the child is among those capable of expression; do you not see that a discerning child's transaction is effective with the permission of his guardian? And the slave has capacity for transaction over himself; he owns it; only he does not have authority for transaction with respect to his master. And appointment as agent is not a transaction concerning his master's right. But acceptance of liability from them is not valid: the child due to deficiency of his capacity, and the slave due to the right of his master. So these rights become obligatory on the principal. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that if the buyer did not know the condition of the seller and then learned that he was a child, a madman, or a restricted slave, then he has the option to rescind the sale, because the buyer entered the contract under the assumption that his rights would attach to the contracting party; when the opposite appears, he has a choice, just as when he becomes aware of a defect.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the text of Al-Hidayah. It establishes the second condition for the agent in a contract of agency: the agent must be sane and must understand the legal implications of the contract. The author explains that the agent acts as the principal's representative in expression; therefore, he must have the capacity to express validly, which requires intellect. The proof is that the agent substitutes for the principal in speech, and valid speech requires understanding. The passage then presents the ruling: a free, sane, adult, or a slave permitted to trade can appoint an agent of similar status. It is also valid to appoint a discerning minor or a restricted slave as agent, but they cannot bear liability; liability rests with the principal. The child's capacity is deficient, and the slave's liability is restricted by the master's right. A narration from Abu Yusuf adds that if a buyer was unaware that the seller (agent) was a minor, insane, or a restricted slave, he has the option to rescind the contract upon discovery, analogous to the option for a defect. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that agency is valid as long as the agent can express the contract, even if he cannot bear financial liability.

Key Terms
أهل العبارة (ahl al-ibārah): A person who is capable of valid legal expression, i.e., sane, discerning, and understands the legal implications of the contract.
محجور (maḥjūr): A person placed under interdiction, such as a minor or a slave restricted from trading, meaning they are prevented from certain legal actions.
عهدة (ʿuhdah): Liability or responsibility for the contract, including warranty and payment obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Agency requires only the capacity for expression (ahliyyat al-ibarah), not full legal capacity; therefore a discerning minor or restricted slave may be an agent, but liability falls on the principal.

Hanafi Validation of Agency by Minors and Slaves
Inter-madhhab dispute on agency capacity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid agency in Hanafi law and apply them to justify appointing a restricted minor or slave as an agent, while contrasting with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Agency requires only the principal’s right to transact and the agent’s capacity for expression—not full legal capacity or liability.

You have likely seen contracts where one person acts on behalf of another. But what if the representative is a child or a slave who cannot be held financially liable? The Hanafi school, in a key dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, holds that such representation is valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that ruling, revealing the precise conditions that make agency work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of valid agency
From the Hanafi proof, list the two conditions: (1) the principal must own the right to transact and be bound by the rulings; (2) the agent must understand the contract and be capable of expressing it.
These conditions focus on capacity to transact and communicate, not on bearing liability.
2
Apply the conditions to a discerning minor
A discerning minor (sabi 'āqil) understands the meaning of sale and can express it. Although his transactions require guardian permission, he is capable of expression—hence condition (2) is met. Condition (1) is met by the principal, who is a free adult.
3
Apply the conditions to a restricted slave
A restricted slave (maḥjūr 'alayh) cannot transact for his master, but he owns the right to transact over himself (e.g., acknowledging debts). Appointing him as agent does not involve the master’s rights. The slave is capable of expression, so both conditions are satisfied.
4
State the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that such agents lack full legal capacity and cannot bear liability. The Hanafis respond that liability is not a condition of agency; the agent only needs to speak the contract. The principal bears all liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi law, a sale by a minor or slave agent is valid and binding on the principal. In Shafi'i law, it is invalid. This shows how different conditions lead to different rulings.

ہوتے ہیں۔ اسی کے قائل امام احمد ہیں۔ امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ مبی مجور علیہ اور عبد مجو عالیہ کو وکیل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ احناف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وکالت کے صحیح ہونے کیلئے دو شرطیں ہیں۔ ایک یہ کہ موکل جب صبی عاقل عبارت بیان کرنے کی لیاقت رکھتا ہے اور وکیل ہونے کیلئے یہی ضروری ہے تو صبی عاقل کا وکیل ہونا درست در جائز ہے اور ربا غلام تو وہ بھی تصرف کا مالک ہے اور اپنی ذات پر تصرف کا حق رکھتا ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر غلام اپنے اوپر کسی کیلئے مال کا قرار کر لے تو آزاد ہونے کے بعد اس پر وہ مال لازم ہو جاتا ہے اور غلام کا اپنے اوپر قصاص اور حدود کا اقرار کرنا درست ہے۔ بہر حال غاہم اپنی ذات پر تصرف کا اہل ہے اگر چہ مولی کے حق میں تصرف کا مالک نہیں ہے اور مؤکل کی طرف سے کسی غلام کو وکیل کرنا اسکے مولیٰ کے حق میں تصرف نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ توکیل کی صحت وکیل کی عبارت اور اہلیت کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتی ہے۔ اور ان دونوں باتوں میں غاصلا آدمی ہونے کی حیثیت سے اصل حریت پر باقی ہے۔ یعنی ان دونوں باتوں میں غلام آزاد کے مانند ہے۔ پس جب غلام اپنی ذات پر تصرف کا مالک ہے اور تو کیل مولی کے حق میں تصرف بھی نہیں تو غلام کو وکیل بنانا درست ہے۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ میں اور غلام پرکوئی ذمہ داری لاگو نہیں ہوگی۔ صبی پر تو اسلئے کہ عدم بلوغ کی وجہ سے اس کی اہلیت اور لیاقت ناقص ہے اور غلام پر اسلئے کہ اس کے مولی کوضر ہوگا۔ لہذا مولی
Exposition: Author of al-Quduri said that if a free, sane, adult, or a slave permitted to trade, or a child who understands the meaning of sale and purchase and is permitted to trade, appoints a person of his own rank as agent, then this appointment is valid. Author of al-Inayah said that when it is permissible to appoint one's equal, then appointing a person of better condition is permissible primarily. Rationally, three scenarios arise, and all three are permissible: appointing an equal, like a free sane adult appointing a free sane adult, or a permitted slave appointing a permitted slave; appointing a person of better condition, like a permitted slave appointing a free sane adult; appointing a person of lesser condition, like a free man appointing a permitted slave. The proof of permissibility for all three is that in all three, the principal owns the right to transact and the agent is among those capable of expression, and any agency where the principal owns the right to transact and the agent is capable of expression is valid. Therefore, the mentioned three scenarios are also valid and permissible. Author of al-Quduri said that if a free, sane, adult appoints a restricted (non-permitted) child who understands the meaning of sale and purchase, or a restricted (non-permitted) slave, as agent, then this appointment is permissible, but the rights of the sale are not attached to them; rather they attach to their principals. Imam Ahmad holds this view. Imam al-Shafi'i said that it is not permissible to appoint a restricted minor or a restricted slave as agent. The proof of the Hanafis is that for the validity of agency there are two conditions: first, that the principal owns the right to transact and rulings become binding upon him; second, that the agent is aware of the meaning and concept of the contract and is capable of expression. Here, praise be to Allah, both conditions are present. Because the principal, being free, sane, adult, owns transactions and rulings of the contract become binding upon him. And the agent is a discerning child, and a discerning child is capable of expression; indeed, the transaction of a discerning child is effective with the permission of his guardian. So when a discerning child has the capacity for expression, and this is sufficient for being an agent, then a discerning child being an agent is correct and permissible. As for the slave, he too owns the right to transact and has the right of transaction over himself. This is why if a slave makes a financial obligation on himself for someone, it becomes binding on him after he becomes free, and a slave's acknowledgment of qisas and hudud upon himself is valid. In short, a slave is capable of transaction over himself, even if he does not own the right to transact with respect to his master, and appointing a slave as agent from the principal is not a transaction regarding his master's right, because the validity of agency relates to the agent's expression and capacity. In these two respects, the slave, as a human being, remains on the original freedom; i.e., in these two respects the slave is like a free man. So when the slave owns the right to transact over himself and agency is not a transaction regarding the master's right, then appointing a slave as agent is valid. However, it is necessary that no liability applies to the child and the slave: to the child due to deficiency of his capacity because of non-adulthood, and to the slave because it would harm his master. Therefore, the master... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of appointing a restricted minor or slave as an agent. The author first clarifies that according to al-Quduri and al-Inayah, it is permissible to appoint one's equal, superior, or inferior as agent, as long as the principal has the right to transact and the agent has capacity for expression. All three scenarios are valid by a logical priority: if equal is allowed, then superior is even more so; and inferior is also allowed due to the presence of the two conditions. The core dispute arises when the agent is a restricted minor or slave who cannot bear liability. Al-Shafi'i rejects this, while the Hanafis (and Imam Ahmad) accept it. The Hanafi proof is that agency requires only two conditions: (1) principal owns the transaction, (2) agent can express the contract. Both are satisfied here. The minor is capable of expression, as seen by the effectiveness of his transactions with guardian's permission. The slave owns transaction over himself and can acknowledge liabilities. Appointing them does not harm the master's right because agency is not a transaction concerning the master. The only limitation is that the liability (ʿuhdah) does not attach to them but to the principal. The text ends mid-sentence discussing the reason. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of focusing on the capacity for expression (ahliyyat al-ibarah) as sufficient for agency, unlike Shafi'i who may require full legal capacity.

Key Terms
ماذون له في التجارة (ma'dhūn lahu fī al-tijārah): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, thereby having capacity to transact within that permission.
مجور عليه (maḥjūr 'alayh): A person under interdiction, i.e., restricted from certain legal actions, such as a minor or a slave without trade permission.
اهلية العبارة (ahliyyat al-ibārah): Capacity for expression; the ability to articulate a contract orally or by equivalent means, sufficient for acting as an agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he owns the right to transact over himself.
B) Because he is permitted by his master.
C) Because his master is present.
D) Because the contract is oral.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, agency is valid when the principal owns the transaction and the agent can express the contract; liability is not an agent's requirement.

Agency in Isqat Contracts: The Agent as Messenger
Explains the second category of agency contracts where the agent is a mere messenger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi classification of agency into two categories and identify which specific contracts make the agent a safir (messenger) by applying the qiyas based on possession (qabd).

Core Idea
In isqat contracts, the agent is a mere messenger because possession is required and the property belongs to the principal.

Agency (wakalah) is not a monolithic concept. The Hanafi school divides it into two categories: contracts where the agent acts as a principal (asil), such as sale, and contracts where the agent is merely a safir (messenger). This second category involves isqat contracts—those that terminate or transfer rights without creating new ownership in the agent. Let's examine the examples and the underlying qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of agency
The passage begins by distinguishing the second category from the first. Note: the first category includes contracts like sale (bay') where the agent is an asil; the second includes isqat contracts where the agent is a safir.
The sulh (compromise) that runs the course of sale belongs to the first category.
2
List the contracts of the second category
The passage names: 'itq 'ala mal (freeing for compensation), kitabah (contract of manumission), and sulh 'an al-inkar (settlement after denial). Then it adds six more: hibah, sadaqah, i'arah, wadi'ah, rahn, and iqrad.
Sulh 'an al-iqrar (settlement after admission) is like sale and belongs to the first category.
3
Understand the reason (ta'lil)
The agent is a safir because the legal ruling (e.g., transfer of ownership) is established by qabd (physical possession), and that possession meets a property already owned by someone else (the principal). Thus the agent cannot be a principal.
This is qiyas: since the effect depends on qabd, and qabd occurs on the principal's property, the agent cannot independently own or transfer rights.
4
Note the exception
Agency for borrowing (istiqrad) is invalid; if a principal sends a mere messenger (rasul) to borrow, it is valid, but proper agency for borrowing is null because it would make the principal own the borrowed item without proper transfer.
This exception highlights the rule: in isqat contracts, the agent's role is limited to conveying possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثانى من اخواته العتق على مال والكتابة والصلح عن الانكار فاما الصلح الذي هو جار مجرى البيع فهو من الضرب الاول و الوكيل بالهبة والتصدق والاعارة والايداع والرهن والاقراض سفير ايضا لان الحكم فيها يثبت بالقبض وانه يلاقى محلا مملوكا للغير فلا يجعل اصيلا وكذا اذا كان الركيل من جانب الملتمس وكذا الشركة والمضاربة الا ان التوكيل بالاستقراض باطل حتى لا يثبت الملك للمؤكل بخلاف الرسالة فيه
And the second category from its counterparts: freeing for compensation, kitabah, and sulh 'an al-inkar. As for sulh that runs the course of sale, it is from the first category. And the agent for hibah, sadaqah, i'arah, wadi'ah, rahn, and iqrad is also a safir, because the ruling in them is established by qabd, and it meets a place owned by another, so he is not made a principal. Similarly, if the agent is from the side of the petitioner, and similarly for shirkah and mudarabah, except that agency for borrowing is invalid, so that ownership is not established for the principal, unlike the case of sending a messenger for borrowing.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah identifies the second category of agency contracts—those that involve 'isqat' or termination of rights. The author lists three examples: freeing a slave for compensation ('itq 'ala mal), kitabah (contract of manumission), and sulh 'an al-inkar (compromise settlement after denial). He then clarifies that sulh which functions like a sale (sulh 'an al-iqrar) belongs to the first category where the agent acts as a principal. Following this, he lists six contracts where the agent is merely a safir (messenger): hibah (gift), sadaqah (charity), i'arah (loan of use), wadi'ah (deposit), rahn (pawn), and iqrad (lending). The reason given is that in these contracts, the legal ruling is established by physical possession (qabd), and that possession occurs on property already owned by someone else (the principal). Therefore, the agent cannot be considered an 'asil' (principal) because the effect of the contract must directly relate to the owner. The author adds that if the agent is appointed by the person seeking the benefit (e.g., the borrower), the same rule applies, and similarly for partnerships (shirkah) and mudarabah. An exception is agency for borrowing (istiqrad), which is invalid because it would make the borrowed property owned by the principal without a valid transfer of ownership, unlike the case where a mere messenger (rasul) is sent to borrow, which is valid.

Key Terms
سفير (safīr): An agent who merely conveys the principal's words/actions without own legal authority; a messenger.
أصيل (aṣīl): An agent who acts as a principal, with rights and responsibilities in the contract.
إسقاط (isqāṭ): Termination or dropping of a right; contracts that cause a right to be extinguished.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical possession; the act of taking hold of property, which is required for the transfer of ownership in certain contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sale (bay')
B) Gift (hibah)
C) Marriage (nikah)
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the second category of agency comprises isqat contracts where the agent is a safir because the legal effect depends on qabd of property owned by the principal.

Agent as Transmitter vs Principal in Hanafi Contracts
Defines when agent is safeer vs asil in various contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contracts where the agent is a safeer (mere transmitter) versus asil (principal), and apply the ruling that agency for borrowing is batil.

Core Idea
In possession-based contracts, the agent is a safeer; in speech-based contracts, the agent is asil.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi classification of agency (wakalah). The key distinction is whether the contract is completed by speech alone or requires possession. Understanding this distinction determines who bears the rights and obligations—the agent or the principal. The passage also examines the special case of agency for borrowing (tawkeel bil-istiqrad), which is ruled batil due to the agent assuming liability while the principal receives the benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Determine whether the contract is concluded by speech alone (like sale) or requires possession for completion (like gift, deposit).
Speech-based contracts do not depend on possession; the offer and acceptance alone create the contract.
2
Determine the agent's role
If the contract is speech-based, the agent is asil (principal) because his speech is his own property. If possession-based, the agent is safeer (mere transmitter) because possession pertains to the principal's property.
The agent's role determines who bears the rights and obligations of the contract.
3
Analyze the exception: agency for borrowing
Although borrowing involves possession, agency for borrowing is batil (void). This is because the agent assumes liability for repayment while the principal gets the loaned property—analogous to selling another's property with the price going to the agent.
The invalidity arises from the agent's liability, not just the possession requirement.
4
Apply the ruling to similar contracts
The passage extends the safeer role to partnership (shirkah) and mudarabah contracts: the agent is a transmitter, and rights attach to the principal.
Other schools may differ; the Hanafi position is consistent within the school.

Source Text

اشرف الہدایہ جلد وہم

پر قبضہ واقع ہوا ہے اسکے اعتبار سے وکیل اجنبی ہے اور جب ان عقود میں وکیل اصیل نہیں ہے تو وہ سفیر اور مالک (مؤکل) کی طرف سے محض کلام نقل کرنے والا ہو گا۔ اور وکیل جب ان عقود میں محض سفیر اور معبر ہے تو کے حقوق وکیل ہی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے نہ کہ موکل کے ساتھ ۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر ہبہ، صدقہ ، عاریہ ، ودیعت، رہن، قرضہ مانگنے والے کی طرف سے وکیل ہو تو بھی وہ سفیر ہے۔ یعنی اس صورت میں بھی حقوق قرض تو میرے لئے ہوگا اور اس کا بدل تجھ پر لازم ہوگا۔ یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کوئی شخص دوسرے سے کہے کہ فلاں آدمی کی فلان چیز فروخت کر دے اس شرط پر کہ اس کا شمن میرے لئے ہوگا۔ حالانکہ یہ تو کیل بہالہیچ باطل مال قرض ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ وکیل کا مال ہلاک ہوگا نہ کہ موکل کا مال اور اگر قرضہ کیلئے کسی کو نیچی بنایا تو درست ہے۔ مثلاً ایک شخص دوسرے سے کہتا ہے کہ مجھ کو فلاں آدمی نے تیرے پاس قرضہ لینے کیلئے بھیجا صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر عقد شرکت یا عقد مضاربت کیلئے کسی کو وکیل کیا تو اس عقد کو موکل کی طرف منسوب کرنا ضروری ہوگا اور حقوق عقد مؤکل کی طرف عود کر یں گے نہ کہ وکیل کی طرف ، وکیل محض سفیر اور مجبر ہو گا۔
Also it will be established in such a place that is owned by someone other than the agent, i.e., the principal. And when the ruling of the mentioned contracts is established in a place owned by someone other than the agent, i.e., the principal, then in these contracts the agent is not the principal. Because from the perspective of the place on which possession occurs, the agent is a stranger. And when the agent is not the principal in these contracts, then he is a mere transmitter and a mere conveyer of speech on behalf of the owner (principal). And when the agent is merely a transmitter and interpreter in these contracts, the rights of the contract will not be attached to the agent. Rather, they will be attached to the principal. In contrast, those contracts that are concluded by speech and are not dependent on possession, like sale etc., in those the agent is the principal because the agent is the principal in speaking and uttering, and the agent's speech is the agent's property. So when the agent's speech is his property and he is the principal in speaking, and due to being the principal in speaking he is the principal in these contracts, then the rights of these contracts will be attached to the agent himself, not to the principal. The author of Hidayah says: If someone is an agent for gift, charity, loan, deposit, pledge, or borrowing, then he is still a transmitter. That is, in this case also the rights attach to the principal, not to the agent. However, one thing is necessary: that agency for borrowing is invalid. That is, if a person says to another, 'Go borrow from so-and-so for me, and you are my agent in borrowing,' then this agency is invalid because in this case the principal has commanded the agent to dispose of another's property. Although commanding disposal of another's property is not permissible. A second reason is that the borrower makes the substitute of the loan obligatory upon his own liability. So the meaning of appointing an agent for borrowing is that the principal tells the agent: 'You make the substitute of the loan, e.g., one hundred rupees, obligatory upon your liability, and its compensation invalid, i.e., the loan property will be for me.' That is, the loan will be for me and its substitute will be obligatory upon you. This becomes like when a person says to another: 'Sell such-and-such thing of so-and-so on the condition that its price will be for me.' Although this agency is absolutely invalid. So analogously, agency for borrowing is also invalid. And when agency for borrowing is invalid, then if the agent borrows, the principal will not be its owner. Rather, the agent himself will be the owner. And if the loaned property is destroyed in the agent's possession, it is the agent's property that is destroyed, not the principal's. And if someone appoints another as a deputy (neeche) for borrowing, it is valid. For example, a person says to another, 'So-and-so has sent me to borrow from you.' This is valid. Thus, if the lender (the one to whom the message is sent) gives the loan, then ownership is established for the one who asked for the loan, i.e., the sender. The author of Hidayah said: If someone appoints an agent for a contract of partnership (shirkah) or mudarabah, then it is necessary to attribute this contract to the principal, and the rights of the contract revert to the principal, not to the agent. The agent is merely a transmitter and a compeled one.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explains the distinction between two types of contracts regarding the role of the agent (wakil). In contracts that are concluded solely by speech (like sale), the agent is considered the principal (asil) because he is the one who utters the offer and acceptance; his speech is his own property. Therefore, the rights and obligations of the contract attach to the agent, not to the principal (muwakkil). However, in contracts that require physical possession for their validity, such as gift (hibah), charity (sadaqah), loan for use (ariyah), deposit (wadi'ah), pledge (rahn), and borrowing (qard), the agent is merely a transmitter (safeer) because the possession is taken by the principal or on his property. In these contracts, the rights attach to the principal. The text then discusses a special case: agency for borrowing (tawkeel bil-istiqrad) is invalid (batil) because it involves commanding the agent to obligate himself to repay while the benefit goes to the principal, which is like selling another's property on the condition that the price goes to the agent. Such an agency is void. If the agent borrows despite this, the money becomes the agent's property. However, if a person is sent as a mere messenger (not agent) to request a loan, it is valid. Finally, the same ruling applies to partnership and mudarabah contracts: the agent is a transmitter.

Key Terms
سفیر (safeer): A mere transmitter or messenger who conveys the contract but does not bear rights/obligations.
اصیل (aseel): The principal party to a contract who bears rights and obligations.
توکیل بالاستقراض (tawkeel bil-istiqraad): Appointing an agent to borrow money on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, an agent appointed to give a gift (hibah) is considered:
A) Asil (principal)
B) Safeer (mere transmitter)
C) Batil (invalid agency)
D) Neither asil nor safeer

Key Takeaway
In possession-dependent contracts, the agent is a mere transmitter (safeer); in speech contracts, the agent is the principal (asil). Agency for borrowing is batil due to the agent's personal liability.

Dispute on Principal's Right to Demand Price from Buyer
Inter-madhhab dispute on principal's demand rights and muqassa in agency sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the principal's right to demand the price and apply the rules of muqassa in agency sales.

Core Idea
The principal cannot demand the price from the buyer because rights belong to the agent as the contractor.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: after an agent sells, can the principal directly demand payment from the buyer? The Hanafi school firmly holds that the principal is a stranger to the contract, while other schools empower him. The text also unravels the intricate rules of debt compensation (muqassa) involving the principal, agent, and buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine the exact legal question: Can the muwakkil (principal) demand the price from the buyer after the agent concludes the sale?
The dispute centers on who holds the rights to the contract.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
Hanafi: The principal is a stranger to the contract; rights belong to the aaqid (agent). Therefore, the buyer may refuse payment to the principal.
Key principle: al-huquuq ila al-aqid (rights go to the contractor).
3
Contrast with opposing schools
Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali hold that rights revert to the principal; thus he can demand payment and the buyer cannot refuse.
This difference arises from different views on whose rights are established by the contract.
4
Examine the exception: voluntary payment
If the buyer voluntarily pays the principal, it is valid, and the agent cannot demand again. However, the agent guarantees the price to the principal in such a case.
The principal's receipt is valid, but the agent remains liable.
5
Apply the muqassa rules
Analyze the compensation scenarios: (1) buyer owes principal → price offset; (2) buyer owes both → offset with principal's debt only; (3) buyer owes only agent → offset according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (agent may discharge).
The agent's power of ibraa (discharge) is key in the third scenario.

اور مشتری کا موکل پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرض ہے تو یہ برابر سرابر ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی نہ مشتری کا موکل پر کچھ رہے گا اور نہ موکل کا مشتری پر کچھ رہے گا اور موکل کو وکیل سے کچھ شمن لینے کا حق بھی حاصل نہ ہو قرض ہے اور ایک ہزار روپیہ وکیل پر
Whether the principal can demand the price from the buyer. [Arabic:] Or if the principal demands the price from the buyer, then the buyer has the right to refuse it to him because he (the principal) is a stranger to the contract and its rights, since rights belong to the contractor. If he (the buyer) pays it to him, it is permitted, and the agent does not have the right to demand it from him again because the very price received is his (the principal's) right and has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it from him and then giving it to him. For this reason, if the buyer has a debt against the principal, compensation (muqassa) occurs. And if the buyer has a debt against both of them (principal and agent), compensation occurs with the principal's debt only, not with the agent's debt. And with the agent's debt alone, compensation occurs according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because he (the agent) has the power to discharge (ibra) from it according to them, but he guarantees the price for the principal in both cases. [Translation:] And if the principal demands the price from the buyer, then the buyer has the option to refuse to give the price to the principal because the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights, since rights revert to the contractor. Then if the buyer gives the price to the principal, it is permissible, and the agent does not have the right to demand it from the buyer again because the very price that the principal has taken possession of is the principal's right and has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it from the principal and then giving it to the principal. For this reason, if the buyer has a debt on the principal, compensation occurs. And if the buyer has a debt on both the principal and the agent, compensation occurs with the principal's debt, not with the agent's debt. And if he has a debt only on the agent, then according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), compensation occurs with the agent's debt because according to them the agent is the owner of discharging (ibra) the buyer from the price, but the agent guarantees the price for the principal in both cases. Explanation: The situation is that if a person appointed someone as an agent for sale, then the agent sold the item. Then if the principal demands the price from the buyer, the buyer has the right to refuse. According to Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad, the buyer is not authorized to refuse, because according to them the rights of the contract revert to the principal, not to the agent. And when according to them the rights revert to the principal, the principal himself is authorized to receive the price. And when the principal is authorized to receive the price, then upon the principal's demand for the price, the buyer has no right to refuse. And according to us, the rights of the contract revert to the contractor, i.e., the agent, and the principal is completely a stranger to the contract and its rights. Therefore, the principal is not authorized to demand the price from the buyer. And when the principal is not authorized to demand the price, but he still demands, then the buyer has the right to refuse to give the price, like a person who greets someone performing wudu, but if he greets, the one performing wudu has the right not to respond. Al-Quduri says: If upon the principal's demand the buyer gives the price to the principal, it is permissible, and in that case the agent does not have the right to demand the price from the buyer again. But note: If someone appointed an agent for a contract of exchange (sarf), the principal's taking possession is not valid. However, because the contract of sarf is only valid by possession, possession in sarf is at the level of offer and acceptance. Therefore, if the agent has the right to accept and the principal accepts, it is not permissible. Similarly, if the right of possession is established for the agent and the principal takes possession, it is also not permissible. And for other sales besides sarf, the evidence for validity of the principal's possession is that the price that the principal took possession of is the principal's right and has reached him. And when that is the case, now there is no benefit in taking it from the principal and giving it to the agent and then the agent giving it to the principal. That is, if we order that the buyer takes from the principal and gives to the agent, then the agent gives to the principal, there is no benefit. What was intended has been achieved: the principal has received his right. Since the price that the principal took possession of is the principal's right, therefore if the buyer has a debt equal to the amount on the principal, compensation occurs. For example, the buyer owes one thousand rupees as price, and the buyer has a loan of one thousand rupees on the principal, then they are set off. That is, nothing remains from the buyer to the principal, nor from the principal to the buyer, and the principal does not have the right to take any price from the agent. Similarly, if the buyer has a debt on both the principal and the agent, compensation occurs with the principal's debt, not with the agent's debt. For example, the buyer owes one thousand rupees as price, and the buyer has a loan of one thousand rupees on the principal and one thousand rupees on the agent.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the principal (muwakkil) can demand the price from the buyer after the agent has sold an item. The Hanafi position, based on the principle that the rights of a contract belong to the one who concluded it (al-aqid), i.e., the agent, holds that the principal is a stranger to the contract. Therefore, if the principal demands the price, the buyer has the right to refuse. However, if the buyer voluntarily pays the principal, it is valid, and the agent cannot demand payment again. The passage then discusses the rules of compensation (muqassa): if the buyer owes a debt to the principal, the price and debt cancel each other proportionally. If the buyer owes debts to both the principal and the agent, only the principal's debt offsets the price. If the buyer owes only the agent, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, compensation occurs with the agent's debt because the agent has the power to discharge the buyer, but the agent remains liable to the principal. The opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the rights of the contract belong to the principal, so the principal can demand payment and the buyer cannot refuse. The text also includes a detailed explanation of the application in the case of a contract of sarf (currency exchange), where the principal's taking possession is invalid because possession is a pillar of the contract. Finally, it provides examples of compensation.

Key Terms
مقاصہ (muqaasah): Compensation or offset of mutual debts; the cancellation of debts to the extent they are equal.
عاقد (aaqid): The one who concludes the contract, i.e., the agent in this context.
ابراء (ibraa): Discharge or release from a debt; the agent can discharge the buyer from the price.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the agent guarantees the price to the principal if the buyer pays the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the principal demands the price from the buyer after the agent has sold, what is the buyer's right?
A) He must pay the principal immediately.
B) He can refuse to pay the principal.
C) He must pay both the principal and the agent.
D) He can choose to pay either the principal or the agent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the rights of a contract belong to the agent, not the principal, so the principal cannot demand payment from the buyer.

intermediate

Principal's Demand for Price: Buyer's Right to Refuse
Ruling: principal cannot demand price; buyer may refuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the principal's demand for the price and explain why the buyer may refuse, citing the key principle and contrasting with other schools.

Core Idea
The principal is a stranger to the contract; the buyer may refuse to pay him.

When an agent sells goods, who owns the rights of the contract? In Hanafi law, the agent is the contracting party, not the principal. This lesson explores the practical consequences: the principal cannot demand payment directly from the buyer. We will examine the ruling, its evidence, and how it differs from other madhahib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key question
Consider: an agent sells goods, then the principal demands the price from the buyer. What are the rights of each party?
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The buyer has the right to refuse payment to the principal. The principal cannot enforce his demand.
This is based on the principle that rights of the contract belong to the contracting party (the agent).
3
Contrast with other schools
Imam Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that the principal owns the contract rights, so the buyer must pay the principal.
This is a clear inter-madhhab difference.
4
Explain the Hanafi evidence (dalil)
The text uses the analogy of a person in wudu: like one who says salam to a person in wudu cannot demand a reply, the principal cannot demand payment because he is a stranger to the contract.
The dalil is based on the nature of agency: the agent is the actual party, not the principal.
5
Consider the exception
If the buyer voluntarily pays the principal, it is valid. The agent cannot demand the price again, as the principal has received his due.
There is no benefit in requiring double transfer.

ترجمہ اور اگر مؤکل نے مشتری سے شمن کا مطالبہ کیا تو مشتری کیلئے مؤکل کوشن دینے سے منع کر دینے کا اختیار ہے اسلئے کہ موکل عقد اور حقوق عقد سے اجنبی ہے کیونکہ حقوق عاقد کی طرف راجح ہوتے ہیں۔ پھر اگر مشتری نے موکل کو شمن دے دیا تو جائز ہے اور وکیل کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہے کہ وہ دوبارہ مشتری سے شمن کا مطالبہ کرے۔ اسلئے کہ نفس شمن جس پر مؤکل نے قبضہ کیا ہے۔ مؤکل ہی کا حق ہے اور وہ مؤکل کو پہنچ گیا ہے اور موکل سے لے کر پھر مؤکل کو دینے میں کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے اور اسی وجہ سے اگر مشتری کا موکل پر قرضہ ہو تو مقاصہ واقع ہو جائے گا اور اگر مشتری کا مؤکل اور وکیل دونوں پر قرضہ ہو تو موکل کے قرضہ سے مقاصہ واقع ہوگا نہ کہ وکیل کے قرضہ سے اور اگر تھا وکیل پر قرضہ ہو تو طرفین کے نزدیک وکیل کے قرضہ سے مقاصہ واقع ہوگا۔ کیونکہ طرفین کے نزدیک وکیل مشتری کوشن سے بری کر دینے کا مالک ہے لیکن وکیل دونوں صورتوں میں موکل کیلئے شمن کا ضامن ہوگا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص نے کسی کو وکیل بالمیچ مقرر کیا۔ پھر وکیل نے کسی کو وہ سامان فروخت کر دیا جس کو بیچنے کا وکیل کیا گیا تھا۔ پس اگر مؤکل نے مشتری سے ثمن کا مطالبہ کیا تو مشتری کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ وہ ثمن دینے سے انکار کر دے۔ حضرت امام شافعی ، امام مالک اور امام احمد کے نزدیک مشتری انکار کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ ان حضرات کے نزدیک حقوق عقد مؤکل کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں نہ کہ وکیل کی طرف اور جب ان کے نزدیک حقوق عقد مؤکل کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں تو موکل ہی مشتری سے شن وصول کرنے کا لوٹتے ہیں
مجاز ہے اور جب مؤکل ہی شمن وصول کرنے کا مجاز ہے تو موکل کے مطالبہ ثمن کرنے پر مشتری کو انکار کا کوئی حق حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ اور ہمارے نزدیک حقوق عقد، عاقد یعنی وکیل کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں اور موکل عقد اور حقوق عقد کے سلسلہ میں بالکل اجنبی ہے۔ اسلئے نو کل مشتری سے شمن کے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب موکل شمن کے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے ۔ مگر اس کے باوجود وہ مطالبہ کرتا ہے تو مشتری کو حق ہے کہ وہ شن دینے سے انکار کر دے جیسے کوئی شخص وضو کرنے والے کو سلام نہ کرے لیکن اس کے باوجود اگر سلام کیا تو متونی کو حق ہے کہ وہ جواب نہ دے۔ صاحب قدوری کہتے ہیں کہ اگر مؤکل کے مطالبہ کرنے پر مشتری نے موکل کوشن دے دیا تو یہ بھی جائز ہے اور اس صورت میں وکیل کیلئے مشتری سے دوبارہ شمن کے مطالبہ کا حق نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن یہ خیال رہے کہ اگر کسی نے کسی کو عقد تصرف کا وکیل کیا تو موکل کا قبضہ کرنا صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن کیونکہ عقد صرف قبضہ سے جائز ہوتا ہے۔ پس عقد صرف میں قبضہ ایجاب وقبول کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ لہذا اگر وکیل کیلئے قبول کا حق ثابت ہو اور مؤکل قبول کرے تو یہ نا جائز ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر قبضہ کا حق وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوا اور مؤکل قبضہ کرلے تو یہ بھی نا جائز ہوگا اور عقد صرف کے علاوہ دوسری بیوع میں موکل کے قبضہ کے بیج ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ثمن جس پر موکل نے قبضہ کیا ہے مؤکل ہی کا حق ہے اور وہ مؤکل کو پہنچ گیا۔ اور جب ایسا ہے تو اب موکل سے لے کر پھر مؤکل کو دینے میں کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے یعنی یہ حکم دیا جائے کہ مشتری مؤکل سے لے کر وکیل کو دے پھر وکیل مؤکل کو دے اس میں کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے جو مقصود تھا وہ حاصل ہو گیا یعنی مؤکل کو اس کا حق پہنچ گیا ہے۔ چونکہ ثمن جس پر موکل نے قبضہ کیا ہے مؤکل ہی کا حق ہے۔ اسلئے اگر مشتری کا بقدر من مؤکل پر قرضہ ہو تو مقاصہ واقع ہو جائے گا۔ مثلاً مشتری کے ذمہ ایک ہزار روپی شمن ہے اور مشتری کا موکل پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرض ہے تو یہ برابر سرابر ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی نہ مشتری کا موکل پر کچھ رہے گا اور نہ موکل کا مشتری پر کچھ رہے گا اور موکل کو وکیل سے کچھ شمن لینے کا حق بھی حاصل نہ ہو گا۔ اسی طرح اگر مشتری کا موکل اور وکیل دونوں پر قرضہ ہو تو موکل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ واقع ہوگا نہ کہ وکیل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ ۔ مثلاً مشتری کے ذمہ ایک ہزار رو پیشن ہے اور مشتری کا ایک ہزار روپیہ موکل پر قرض ہے اور ایک ہزار روپیہ وکیل پر قرض ہے تو ایک ہزار روپی شمن اور وہ ایک ہزار روپیہ جو موکل پر قرض ہے ان کے درمیان مقاصہ واقع ہو گا۔ یعنی مؤکل اور مشتری دونوں برابر سرابر ہو جائیں گے۔ نہ موکل کا مشتری پر کچھ رہے گا اور نہ مشتری کا موکل پر کچھ رہے گا اور مؤکل کو وکیل سے کچھ من وغیرہ لینے کا حق بھی نہ ہوگا۔
اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مقاصہ نام ہے ابراء بالعوض کا یعنی بری کر دینے کا نام مقاصہ ہے اور اس کو ابراء بغیر العوض پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔ پس اگر مؤکل اور وکیل دونوں نے ایک ساتھ مشتری کو شمن سے بری کیا تو مشتری مؤکل کے بری کرنے سے بری ہوتا ہے نہ کہ وکیل کے بری کرنے سے۔ چنانچہ اس صورت میں موکل ، وکیل سے ثمن وغیرہ کچھ رجوع کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا۔ بہر حال جب ابراء بغیر العوض میں موکل کا بری کرنا معتبر ہے نہ کہ وکیل کا تو ابراء بالعوض یعنی مقاصہ میں بھی مؤکل کے قرضہ کے عوض ابراء اور مقاصہ معتبر ہوگا نہ کہ وکیل کے قریضہ کے عوض ۔
دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں اگر وکیل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیں تو اس صورت میں اس کی ضرورت پڑے گی کہ وکیل اپنے پاس سے مؤکل کوشن ادا کرے اور اگر مؤکل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیں تو اس کی ضرورت پیش نہ آئے گی ۔ پس مسافت کو کم کرنے کیلئے موکل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیا گیا اور اگر مشتری کا قرضہ تنہا وکیل پر ہو تو اس صورت میں طرفین کے نزد یک وکیل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ واقع ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وکیل طرفین کے نزدیک مشری کو شمن سے بلا عوض بری کر دینے کا مالک ہے اور جب وکیل بلاعوض مشتری کوشن سے بری کر دیتے کا مالک ہے تو مقاصہ یعنی ابراء بالعوض کا مالک بدرجہ اولی ہو گا ۔ پس ایراء بالعوض کو ابراء بلاعوض پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ وکیل مشتری کو بلاعوض بری کرے یا بعوض بری کرے دونوں صورتوں میں وکیل اپنے مؤکل کیلئے مقدار شمن کا ضامن ہوگا۔
رہی یہ بات کہ طرفین کے نزدیک وکیل بلا عوض مشتری کو شمن سے بری کر دینے کا مالک کیوں ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ وکیل کے حق میں ابراء نام ہے قبضہ کے حق کو ساقط کرنے کا اور قبضہ خالص وکیل کا حق ہے۔ چنانچہ مؤکل اگر وکیل کو قبضہ کرنے سے منع کرنا چاہے تو اس کو یہ اختیار نہ ہوگا اور اگر مؤکل خود قبضہ کرنا چاہے تو اس کو یہ حق بھی نہیں ہے۔ پس وکیل ہمشتری کو قبضہ ثمن سے بری کرنے میں خالص اپنے حق کو ساقط کرنے والا ہے اور اپنا حق ساقط کرنے کا ہر شخص مجاز ہے۔ لہذا وکیل مشتری کو بری کرنے کا مجاز ہے اور جب وکیل بلا عوض مشتری کو بری کرنے کا مالک نے تو بالعوض بری کرنے یعنی مقاصہ کرنے کا حق ساقط کرنے کا
بدرجہ اولی مالک و مختار ہوگا۔ مگر چونکہ وکیل نے قبضہ کرنے کا حق ساقط کر دیا۔ اور اسکی وجہ سے مؤکل کیلئے شن وصول کرنے کا دروازہ بند ہو گیا ۔ کیونکہ مؤکل کو مشتری کی طرف سے شمن پر قبضہ کرنے کا کوئی حق نہیں ہوتا اسلئے وکیل، موکل کیلئے مقدار ثمن کا ضامن ہوگا۔ جیسے راہن اگر اپنے عبد مرہون کو آزاد کر دے تو وہ آزاد ہو جائیگا۔ کیونکہ عہد مرہون راہن ہی کی ملک ہے۔ اگر چہ مرتہن کے قبضہ میں ہے لیکن چونکہ غلام کی مالیت سے مرتین کیلئے اپنا قرضہ وصول کرنا ناممکن ہو گیا۔ اسلئے راہن، مرتہن کیلئے مقدار قرض کا ضامن ہوگا۔ حضرت امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا کہ وکیل مشتری کو بلاعوض بری کر دینے کا مالک نہیں ہے کیونکہ مشتری کے ذمہ میں جوشن ہے وہ مؤکل کی ملک ہے پس وکیل کا مشتری کو بری کرنا غیر یعنی مؤکل کی ملک میں بلا اجازت تصرف کرنا ہے اور بلا اجازت کسی کی ملک میں
Book of Agency. Translation: And if the principal demanded the price from the buyer, the buyer has the right to refuse to give the price to the principal, because the principal is a stranger to the contract and the rights of the contract, since the rights of the contract revert to the contracting party. Then if the buyer gave the price to the principal, it is permissible, and the agent does not have the right to demand the price again from the buyer, because the very price that the principal took possession of is the right of the principal, and it has reached the principal, and there is no benefit in taking it from the principal and then giving it back to the principal. And for this reason, if the buyer has a debt against the principal, a set-off (muqassa) will occur. And if the buyer has a debt against both the principal and the agent, the set-off will occur with the principal's debt, not with the agent's debt. And if the buyer only has a debt against the agent, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the set-off will occur with the agent's debt, because according to the two shaykhs, the agent has the right to discharge the buyer from the price, but in both cases the agent will be guarantor for the price for the principal. Explanation: The issue is that if a person appointed someone as an agent for sale, and the agent sold the goods that he was authorized to sell. Then if the principal demanded the price from the buyer, the buyer has the right to refuse to give the price. According to Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad, the buyer is not permitted to refuse, because according to them, the rights of the contract revert to the principal, not to the agent. And when according to them the rights of the contract revert to the principal, then the principal is the one authorized to receive the price from the buyer. And when the principal is authorized to receive the price, then when the principal demands the price, the buyer has no right to refuse. And according to us, the rights of the contract revert to the contracting party, i.e., the agent, and the principal is completely a stranger in relation to the contract and the rights of the contract. Therefore, the principal is not authorized to demand the price from the buyer. And when the principal is not authorized to demand the price, but he still demands it, the buyer has the right to refuse to give the price, like if a person does not say salam to someone performing wudu, but if he does say salam, the person in wudu has the right not to reply. The author of al-Quduri says that if upon the principal's demand, the buyer gives the price to the principal, this is also permissible, and in this case, the agent does not have the right to demand the price again from the buyer. But note that if someone appointed an agent for a contract of disposition, the principal's taking possession is not valid. However, because a contract of currency exchange (sarf) is only valid with possession, possession in a sarf contract is at the level of offer and acceptance. Therefore, if the agent has the right of acceptance and the principal accepts, it is invalid. Similarly, if the agent has the right of possession and the principal takes possession, it is also invalid. And in other sales besides sarf, the evidence for the validity of the principal's possession is that the price over which the principal took possession is the right of the principal, and it has reached the principal. And when it is so, there is no benefit in taking it from the principal and then giving it to the agent and then the agent giving it to the principal; the purpose has been achieved, i.e., the principal has received his right. Since the price that the principal took possession of is the right of the principal, therefore if the buyer has a debt against the principal equal to the price, a set-off will occur. For example, the buyer owes one thousand rupees as price and the buyer has a claim of one thousand rupees against the principal, then they will offset each other. That is, the buyer will owe nothing to the principal and the principal will owe nothing to the buyer, and the principal will have no right to claim any price from the agent. Similarly, if the buyer has debts against both the principal and the agent, the set-off will occur with the principal's debt, not with the agent's debt. For example, the buyer owes one thousand rupees as price and the buyer has a claim of one thousand rupees against the principal and a claim of one thousand rupees against the agent. Then the set-off will occur between the one thousand rupees price and the one thousand rupees that is a claim against the principal. That is, the principal and the buyer will be equal; the principal will owe nothing to the buyer and the buyer will owe nothing to the principal, and the principal will have no right to claim any price or anything from the agent. The evidence for this is that muqassa (set-off) is a name for ibra bi al-iwad (discharge with compensation), and it is analogized to ibra bi la iwad (discharge without compensation). So if both the principal and the agent together discharged the buyer from the price, the buyer is discharged by the principal's discharge, not by the agent's discharge. Accordingly, in this case, the principal is not permitted to claim back the price or anything from the agent. In any case, when in ibra bi la iwad, the principal's discharge is valid, not the agent's, then in ibra bi al-iwad, i.e., muqassa, the discharge and set-off will be valid in exchange for the principal's debt, not in exchange for the agent's debt. The second evidence is that in the mentioned case, if we establish set-off with the agent's debt, then it will require that the agent pay the price to the principal from his own pocket. And if we establish set-off with the principal's debt, this need does not arise. So to reduce difficulty, set-off is established with the principal's debt. And if the buyer's debt is only against the agent, then according to the two shaykhs, set-off will occur with the agent's debt, because according to the two shaykhs, the agent has the right to discharge the buyer from the price without compensation. And when the agent has the right to discharge the buyer without compensation, then he has the right to muqassa, i.e., discharge with compensation, a fortiori. So ibra bi al-iwad is analogized to ibra bi la iwad. However, it is necessary that whether the agent discharges the buyer without compensation or with compensation, in both cases the agent will be guarantor for the amount of the price for his principal. As for why according to the two shaykhs the agent has the right to discharge the buyer without compensation, the answer is that for the agent, ibra (discharge) means dropping the right of possession, and possession is exclusively the right of the agent. Thus, if the principal wants to prevent the agent from taking possession, he does not have that right, and if the principal wants to take possession himself, he also does not have that right. So by discharging the buyer from possession of the price, the agent is dropping his own exclusive right, and every person is permitted to drop his own right. Therefore, the agent is permitted to discharge the buyer. And when the agent has the right to discharge without compensation, he has the right to discharge with compensation, i.e., to do muqassa, a fortiori. But because the agent dropped his right of possession, thereby closing the door for the principal to receive the price (since the principal has no right to take possession of the price from the buyer), the agent becomes guarantor for the amount of the price for the principal. For example, if a pledgor frees his pledged slave, the slave becomes free, because the pledged slave is the property of the pledgor, even though it is in the possession of the pledgee. But because it became impossible for the pledgee to recover his debt from the value of the slave, the pledgor becomes guarantor for the amount of the debt for the pledgee. Imam Abu Yusuf said that the agent is not entitled to discharge the buyer without compensation, because the price that is in the buyer's liability is the property of the principal. So the agent's discharging the buyer is disposing in the property of another (the principal) without permission, and disposing in someone else's property without permission
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on the relationship between principal, agent, and buyer after a sale. The text begins by stating the ruling: if the principal demands the price from the buyer, the buyer may refuse to pay him because the principal is a stranger to the contract—the rights of the contract belong to the agent. This is the Hanafi position. However, if the buyer does pay the principal, it is valid and the agent cannot demand payment again, as the principal has already received his due. The text then contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools, who hold that the principal owns the rights of the contract and therefore can demand payment directly. The Hanafi school bases its view on the principle that the rights of a contract belong to the contracting party (the agent). The text then explains the implications for set-off (muqassa): if the buyer owes the principal a debt, the price and debt are set off, so neither owes the other. If the buyer owes both principal and agent, set-off occurs with the principal's debt only, to avoid unnecessary transfer. If the buyer owes only the agent, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two shaykhs), set-off with the agent's debt occurs because they hold that the agent can discharge the buyer without compensation. However, Abu Yusuf differs on this point, asserting that the agent cannot discharge the buyer without compensation because the price is the property of the principal. The text concludes that in all cases, if the agent discharges the buyer (whether for compensation or not), the agent becomes guarantor for the price to the principal, analogous to a pledgor who frees a pledged slave and guarantees the debt.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakīl): Agent appointed by principal (muwakkil) to perform a specific act, such as sale.
مؤکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent; he is a stranger to the contract and cannot interfere with rights that belong exclusively to the agent.
مقاصہ (muqāṣṣa): Set-off of mutual debts; here, it applies between the price owed by the buyer and debts owed by the principal to the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an agent sells goods and the principal demands the price from the buyer, what is the buyer's right?
A) The buyer must pay the principal.
B) The buyer may refuse to pay the principal.
C) The buyer must pay the agent, not the principal.
D) The buyer may choose to pay either the principal or the agent.

Key Takeaway
The principal is a stranger to the contract; the buyer may refuse to pay him, but if he pays voluntarily, it is valid and the agent cannot reclaim.

8 min
Agent's Discharge of Buyer and Set-Off: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's discharge and set-off rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the Hanafi position on the agent's right to discharge the buyer, identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and apply the rules of set-off (muqassa) when debts involve the agent.

Core Idea
The agent can discharge the buyer without compensation because the right of possession belongs exclusively to the agent.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the principal cannot demand the price. But can the agent voluntarily release the buyer from the obligation to pay? This lesson addresses that question and the resulting set-off rules. There is a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the agent cannot discharge without compensation, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold he can. We will examine the evidence and the practical implications for set-off.

Guided Path
1
State the two-shaykhs' position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the agent can discharge the buyer from the price without compensation (ibra bi la iwad).
This is because possession is the agent's exclusive right; dropping it is like dropping any personal right.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi opponent
Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees: the agent cannot discharge without compensation because the price is the property of the principal.
For Abu Yusuf, the agent's discharge is a disposition of another's property without permission.
3
Analyze the evidence for the two-shaykhs
They analogize to a pledgor freeing a pledged slave: the pledgor owns the slave, so freeing is valid, but he becomes guarantor for the pledgee's debt. Similarly, the agent drops his right of possession, but becomes guarantor for the principal.
The key is that the right being dropped is the agent's own right, not the principal's ownership.
4
Explain the set-off rules
If the buyer owes only the agent, set-off with the agent's debt occurs because the agent can also discharge with compensation. But if the buyer owes both principal and agent, set-off is with the principal's debt only.
This avoids requiring the agent to pay the principal from his own pocket.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs: the agent can discharge, and set-off with the agent's debt is valid when the buyer owes only the agent.
The text presents this as the operative view.
